    Streamlining Linux System Diagnostics: An Open-Source, Web-Based Approach

    To bridge this gap, my friend and colleague developed an open-source solution called Linux AIO Performance Checker.

    This tool is designed for real-time diagnostics and performance issue resolution, rather than long-term monitoring. It is particularly useful for scenarios where actual monitoring metrics are collected at higher (e.g. 5 minute) intervals.

    The automated script uses a range of Linux diagnostic tools, categorized as follows:

    • CPU Diagnostics: mpstat, pidstat, uptime
    • Memory Diagnostics: vmstat, free
    • Storage Diagnostics: iostat, df -h, lsblk -f, parted -l, pvdisplay, vgdisplay, lvdisplay, pvscan, vgscan, lvscan, ls -l /dev/mapper, iotop
    • Generic OS Information: date, top, ps -H, sar, os-release, last installed updates

    The script automates the execution of these diagnostic tools at certain intervals, capturing and parsing their output into a structured format. This eliminates the need for manual command-line operations and streamlines the diagnostic process.

    Once the data is captured, a web-based interface serves as the visualization layer, employing charts and tables to present real-time metrics intuitively. Users can interact with the data to gain immediate insights into system performance and issues.

    Lets take a look:

    Download the diagnostic script onto the Linux server/client OS:

    wget https://github.com/samatild/LinuxAiOPerf/releases/download/1.10/linux_aio_perfcheck.tgz

    Unpack it:

    tar xfz linux_aio_perfcheck.tgz

    Make it executable:

    chmod +x linux_aio_perfcheck.sh

    Run it:

    sudo ./linux_aio_perfcheck.sh

    Run Options #

    • Collect Live Data: Collects data immediately over a timespan ranging from 10 to 900 seconds. This is useful when the problem is happening in real-time.
    • Collect Data via Watchdog: Sets up a watchdog that monitors resource utilization. Both Auto and Manual modes are available.
    • Collect Data via Cron: Sets up a cron job based on user instructions. There are two different cron jobs: Recurrent will repeat the collection based on user selection, while Not Recurrent will trigger the data collection at a specific time.

    Specify the capture duration. For this demo I will be using 5 minutes:

    def cpu_stress(): while True: for _ in range(10000): 3.141592 * 2

    def memory_stress(): arr = array.array(‘I’, (0 for i in range(2**25))) while True: arr.reverse()

    def disk_io_stress(): with tempfile.TemporaryDirectory() as tempdir: while True: # Write a 100MB file with open(os.path.join(tempdir, “stress_test_file”), “wb”) as f: for _ in range(100): f.write(os.urandom(1024 * 1024)) # Write 1MB of random bytes

            # Read the file back into memory
        with open(os.path.join(tempdir, "stress_test_file"), "rb") as f:
            while f.read(1024 * 1024):  # Read 1MB at a time
                pass

    if name == “main”: for _ in range(multiprocessing.cpu_count()): multiprocessing.Process(target=cpu_stress).start() multiprocessing.Process(target=memory_stress).start() multiprocessing.Process(target=disk_io_stress).start() " role=“button” style=“color:#D4D4D4;display:none” tabindex=“0”>``` import multiprocessing import array import os import tempfile def cpu_stress(): while True: for _ in range(10000): 3.141592 * 2 def memory_stress(): arr = array.array(‘I’, (0 for i in range(2**25))) while True: arr.reverse() def disk_io_stress(): with tempfile.TemporaryDirectory() as tempdir: while True: # Write a 100MB file with open(os.path.join(tempdir, “stress_test_file”), “wb”) as f: for _ in range(100): f.write(os.urandom(1024 * 1024)) # Write 1MB of random bytes # Read the file back into memory with open(os.path.join(tempdir, “stress_test_file”), “rb”) as f: while f.read(1024 * 1024): # Read 1MB at a time pass if name == "main": for _ in range(multiprocessing.cpu_count()): multiprocessing.Process(target=cpu_stress).start() multiprocessing.Process(target=memory_stress).start() multiprocessing.Process(target=disk_io_stress).start()

This is a sample of what we see. The actual amount of data includes, but is not limited to:

- **CPU**: %usr, %nice, %sys, %iowait, %irq, %soft, %steal, %guest, %gnice, %idle
- **Memory**: swpd, free, buff, cache, inactive, active
- **Disk**: tps, Blk\_read/s, Blk\_wrtn/s, Blk\_dscd/s, Blk\_w+d/s, Blk\_read, Blk\_wrtn, Blk\_dscd, Blk\_w+d, r/s, w/s, d/s, f/s, sec/s, rsec/s, wsec/s, dsec/s, rqm/s, rrqm/s, wrqm/s, drqm/s, %rrqm, %wrqm, %drqm, areq-sz, rareq-sz, wareq-sz, dareq-sz, await, r\_await, w\_await, d\_await, f\_await, aqu-sz, %util
- **Network**: rxpck/s, txpck/s, rxkB/s, txkB/s, rxcmp/s, txcmp/s, rxmcst/s, %ifutil

Pidstat is available for each timestamp, with highlights for the biggest resource consumers:

<figure class="wp-block-image size-full">![](https://cdn.porotnikov.com/media/2023/10/03224750/image-11.jpg)</figure>TOP metrics are also available for each timestamp:

<figure class="wp-block-image size-full">![](https://cdn.porotnikov.com/media/2023/10/03224807/image-11-1.jpg)</figure>I/O stats are displayed for each timestamp via IOtop.

<figure class="wp-block-image size-full">![](https://cdn.porotnikov.com/media/2023/10/03224828/image-11-2.jpg)</figure>As you can see, the Linux AIO Performance Checker is quite a comprehensive tool for real-time Linux system diagnostics. It not only automates the data collection process but also provides an intuitive web-based interface for data visualization, making it a valuable asset for IT professionals. The best part is that the author constantly adds new metrics, features, and improves the tool.
